<div id="review_145587337" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/145587337" />
    <a title="Simon" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1071724-simon"><img alt="Simon" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1242398665p2/1071724.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/145587337?book_show_action=true">Feb 03, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Simon" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Simon" href="/user/show/1071724-simon">Simon</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/10497695-little-big">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1071724-simon?shelf=fantasy-masterworks">fantasy-masterworks</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1071724-simon?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer145587337" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11078944484165523853">This is one of those books that is hard to talk about. Maybe best to describe by analogy. <br/><br/>So imagine a tangled ball of wool with which you are following a strand as it winds its way in around the other strands, in and out of the tangle until eventually you find the other end of the thread, somewhere not too far from where you started.<br/><br/>The narrative flows a bit like that. It nips back and forwards in time, hops from one character to another, spanning several generations of a sprawling family as we</span>
  <span id="freeText11078944484165523853" style="display:none">This is one of those books that is hard to talk about. Maybe best to describe by analogy. <br /><br />So imagine a tangled ball of wool with which you are following a strand as it winds its way in around the other strands, in and out of the tangle until eventually you find the other end of the thread, somewhere not too far from where you started.<br /><br />The narrative flows a bit like that. It nips back and forwards in time, hops from one character to another, spanning several generations of a sprawling family as we gradually find out about their <i>tale</i>, the purpose to which the seemingly random and insignificant events, both happy and tragic, turn out to be all part of some grand plan for the future.<br /><br />An ambitious and often confusing work that requires the reader to take the hints and fill in the gaps in order to make more sense of the story. The lazy, meandering narrative requires a strong prose style to carry it off, to engage the reader whilst the tension slackens and, for the most part, Crowley is up to the job. But only just; he's not quite one of the prose greats in who's writing you can lose yourself no matter what the subject matter. <br /><br />At times I found it slightly hard work, could only read short sections in one sitting, which is why it took me so long to finish. But my respect for the novel grew as it became apparent what the author was trying to do and how cleverly the themes recurred throughout the story as well as in the narrative structure itself. If you read this, in the right frame of mind, with patience and are willing to submerge yourself in Crowley's world, you will likely enjoy this book very much.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11078944484165523853" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_202620019" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/202620019" />
    <a title="colleen the convivial curmudgeon" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1209800-colleen-the-convivial-curmudgeon"><img alt="colleen the convivial curmudgeon" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1572581488p2/1209800.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/202620019?book_show_action=true">Aug 27, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="colleen the convivial curmudgeon" class="user" itemprop="url" name="colleen the convivial curmudgeon" href="/user/show/1209800-colleen-the-convivial-curmudgeon">colleen the convivial curmudgeon</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1209800-colleen-the-convivial-curmudgeon?shelf=group-read">group-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1209800-colleen-the-convivial-curmudgeon?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1209800-colleen-the-convivial-curmudgeon?shelf=magical-realism">magical-realism</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer202620019" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4580611385559468044">I had pretty decent hopes for this book, and maybe that's lent itself, a bit, to the air of disappointment I was left with... but let's start at the beginning.<br/><br/>The prose style is lyrical and others have described it as 'dream-like' - something with which I can agree. At first I had a hard time getting into it, but once I sort of settled into the style I rather enjoyed it as it set up the story of the Bramble-Drinkwaters ('cause, really, even though the cover say it's Smoky's tale it's not, really</span>
  <span id="freeText4580611385559468044" style="display:none">I had pretty decent hopes for this book, and maybe that's lent itself, a bit, to the air of disappointment I was left with... but let's start at the beginning.<br /><br />The prose style is lyrical and others have described it as 'dream-like' - something with which I can agree. At first I had a hard time getting into it, but once I sort of settled into the style I rather enjoyed it as it set up the story of the Bramble-Drinkwaters ('cause, really, even though the cover say it's Smoky's tale it's not, really, imo). <br /><br />The story of Violet and the first Drinkwater, and then Daily Alice and Smoky, and their children and extended family, all touched in some strange way by the fae, and the crazy House, half-real and half-imagined, all a part of the Tale, as we're told over and over, and I went along for the ride as we go back and forth in time and place and character, parts feeling in some antiquated past and parts in some ill-defined future.<br /><br />But a large part of the problem, for me, is that I never really connected with the characters. Perhaps it's because of the generational spanning story and the constant backing and forthing, but I never really 'got' most of the characters - and those ones that I did like spending time with the most, like Smoky and Alice, we sort of abandon for a huge chunk of the meandering middle-portion. (And, oh gods, but how it did meander.)<br /><br />And because I didn't connect with them I couldn't quite care all that much about some of the dramas. I couldn't grok why, for instance, when <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Alice found out about Smoky, her husband, having a brief affair with Sophie, her sister, she was just "well, it's not the first time it's happened in the world."  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> <br /><br />And some parts were just squicky. I mean, I sort of turned a blind-eye when <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[George slept with his cousin Sophie, but, later, when he admits to sleeping with Sylvie whom he suspects is his daughter? WTF?! <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />And yet none of these things are treated with anything except a sort of off-handed acceptance.<br /><br />And maybe that's one thing that bothered me about the characters - their passivity. Because they're part of the Tale they sort of just accept things as happening for a reason, and aren't very proactive. And even Auberon, in his attempt to be proactive, ends up being pretty passive and then just <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[falling apart <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>.<br /><br />So there's that. And then there's the glimpses of plot-line we get that I never really felt like I got a full grasp on. I mean, what <i>was</i> the deal with Eigenblick, or whatever, in the end? <br /><br />Going back to the meandering middle part - we spent pages and chapters and aeons with Auberon and Sylvie in the City, when most of the stuff I was most interested in - concerning Ariel on one end and Smoky on the other - seemed mentioned and sort of glossed over.<br /><br />Like Ariel's first trip to the House. And what in the hell happened between Auberon's going home the first time and then being back in the City?<br /><br />I just kept feeling like certain parts were belabored beyond all reckoning and the parts I wanted to know more about were just skipped.<br /><br />And I don't know how I feel about the ending. On one hand it's kind of bitter-sweet and fitting for the story, but, on the other hand, I couldn't help but feeling "That's it? But what about this other thing over here, and that thing over there, and... and... what?"<br /><br />I don't know. It's a book I sort of feel bad for not liking more. It had it's moments and it had a certain haunting beauty, but it also had, like, 250 too many pages. Maybe it's one of those stories that a discussion (since I read it for a group read) will change my perspective on. Maybe I just need more time and distance... but, often, I end up disliking things more after time and distance, not less.<br /><br />But, then, I suppose all things are possible... so who knows? </span>
  <a data-text-id="4580611385559468044" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_67825930" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/67825930" />
    <a title="Alex" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/766382-alex"><img alt="Alex" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1270092224p2/766382.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/67825930?book_show_action=true">Aug 17, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alex" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alex" href="/user/show/766382-alex">Alex</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer67825930" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13812908122582257687">This is the third time I've read this book. Why keep reading a book that I've only (begrudgingly) given two stars too? Because every time I've finished it I did so loathing it, but as time passed I always forgot why I loathed it and became slightly convinced that it was me, and not the book, that I hadn't read it carefully enough, or thought about it properly, that there was some thing that could easily be removed that once I figured it out would leave me honestly loving a book I'd only felt lik</span>
  <span id="freeText13812908122582257687" style="display:none">This is the third time I've read this book. Why keep reading a book that I've only (begrudgingly) given two stars too? Because every time I've finished it I did so loathing it, but as time passed I always forgot why I loathed it and became slightly convinced that it was me, and not the book, that I hadn't read it carefully enough, or thought about it properly, that there was some thing that could easily be removed that once I figured it out would leave me honestly loving a book I'd only felt like I should love. <br /><br />As it turns out the above sentiments were totally delusional. I am never going to like this book. Ever. Why? Because while I think someone can make a case that in structure (maybe.... honestly I thought this book suffered from "Oh shit I'm running out of pages, better wrap this sucker up!") and language and theme this may actually be a very good book, the characters who inhabit it, in their inhumanity (and maybe that's because they're fey touched or maybe it's because Crowley paints them all as weird ideal types that you'd never actually want to be or be around) and solitude (no one in this book ever touches or knows another person. They're all terribly terribly alone. They might become obsessed, but there's always a million walls between people, even in "love" (which emotion is never believable, except, maybe at first, with Auberon and Sylvie)), slowly drove me to a state of such dislike that around book four I realized that I hated them and within book six I couldn't even try to read carefully and just sprinted for the (stupid) ending. <br /><br />Two random points:<br />1) I felt that it was sort of gross that all of the non-white characters are painted as being sort of feral and homeless, or being these fierce sexual creatures (read: primitive), or in most every case servants. Just all these dumb stereotypes. <br />2) Man I am fucking tired of literary incest. What's the point? What's it supposed to say? Am I supposed to be shocked? Is it supposed to rattle my uber-bourgeoisie cage and get me to, like, really think about the conventional mores that entrap my small mind? Ugh. </span>
  <a data-text-id="13812908122582257687" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer6448303930022446902">4.5 stars, it's a highly imaginative book, even though it might be a bit difficult to get into. Still I admire the author for respecting readers' intelligence. He didn't tell you who is supposed to be who, what is supposed to be what straight away as if he thinks we readers are all idiots who have to be told 1+1=2 other than figuring things out on our own.<br/><br/>Update: 11/04/2014:<br/><br/>It's still a highly imaginative novel about faeries and the mysterious Drinkwater Family, of Edgewood. Generation after gen</span>
  <span id="freeText6448303930022446902" style="display:none">4.5 stars, it's a highly imaginative book, even though it might be a bit difficult to get into. Still I admire the author for respecting readers' intelligence. He didn't tell you who is supposed to be who, what is supposed to be what straight away as if he thinks we readers are all idiots who have to be told 1+1=2 other than figuring things out on our own.<br /><br />Update: 11/04/2014:<br /><br />It's still a highly imaginative novel about faeries and the mysterious Drinkwater Family, of Edgewood. Generation after generation, the fate of the Drinkwater Family (some of the family members can travel to the faeries land, some can communicate with them, some can see them but some can't) is entangled with the faeries race. It's a family saga which is capable of holding its readers spellbound.<br /><br />The novel is not flawless: for many readers, the book may appear to be too uneventful, I also noticed the ending is too subtle and rushed. And to many readers, who are used to the Fantasy Adventure genre (e.g. Harry Potter) and are expecting to see a magical war between humans and faeries, they will definitely be disappointed. <br /><br />Yes, war and conflicts are mentioned in the book, so does magic and supernatural power. But how the battles are won (or lost) is nothing like you might imagine; and the magic works in an elegant, subtle and unusual way.<br /><br />It's a story not unlike <i> One Hundred Years of Solitude</i>, just not as dramatic. It's delightful to see a novel about faeries and magic carrying itself with so much airy grace and charm. I will definitely recommend you to read this book. </span>
  <a data-text-id="6448303930022446902" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer10256582589152951586">This book is like life to me. A dangerous statement, but true! It has the feel of all the moments of my life as it unfolds. It has all the wisdom and subtle instruction by example that is necessary for a rich and various life. It limns many of the other layers of life that are left out of "realist" fiction, and so it's been called fantasy, and until recently that is the section where you would always find this book. But this is reality fiction, and it's hard for me to imagine a person whose actu</span>
  <span id="freeText10256582589152951586" style="display:none">This book is like life to me. A dangerous statement, but true! It has the feel of all the moments of my life as it unfolds. It has all the wisdom and subtle instruction by example that is necessary for a rich and various life. It limns many of the other layers of life that are left out of "realist" fiction, and so it's been called fantasy, and until recently that is the section where you would always find this book. But this is reality fiction, and it's hard for me to imagine a person whose actual life being lived would not be enriched by reading this book.<br /><br />There's a great blurb by Harold Bloom on the back cover of one edition which says something to the effect that it's as if this book has always existed, as if it wasn't even written but has always been. Indeed.</span>

<div id="review_2921812866" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2921812866" />
    <a title="Amanja" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/55422230-amanja"><img alt="Amanja" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1563173728p2/55422230.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2921812866?book_show_action=true">Jan 17, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Amanja" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Amanja" href="/user/show/55422230-amanja">Amanja</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2921812866" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6586389410483700273">This is the spoiler free review for Little, Big by John Crowley. If you would like to read the very long and somewhat confusing synopsis and spoiler full review please visit <a target='_blank' rel='noopener nofollow' href='https://amanjareads.com/2020/01/23/little-big-american-fantasy-novel-review-with-spoilers/'>https://amanjareads.com/2020/01/23/li...</a><br/><br/>I'm calling Little, Big an American fantasy novel simply because it needs it's own genre. It's closest to fantasy but it's not hard fantasy with magic wands or full shapeshifters. It's more like magical realism but the magic isn't accidental or incidental.<br/><br/>It's fantasy set in the early </span>
  <span id="freeText6586389410483700273" style="display:none">This is the spoiler free review for Little, Big by John Crowley. If you would like to read the very long and somewhat confusing synopsis and spoiler full review please visit <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://amanjareads.com/2020/01/23/little-big-american-fantasy-novel-review-with-spoilers/">https://amanjareads.com/2020/01/23/li...</a><br /><br />I'm calling Little, Big an American fantasy novel simply because it needs it's own genre. It's closest to fantasy but it's not hard fantasy with magic wands or full shapeshifters. It's more like magical realism but the magic isn't accidental or incidental.<br /><br />It's fantasy set in the early 1900s in anywhere middle America. It delves into the dichotomy of the little town verses the big city but does not linger there. The setting is largely symbolic and purposefully non-specific.<br /><br />So is any use of magic, protections, curses, changelings, fairies, interdimensional travel, or any other strange fantasy-esque doings. Little, Big makes its own rules and runs its own genre defying plots wherever it pleases.<br /><br />The book follows the entire history of the Drinkwater family starting with Smoky and Daily Alice but going to her previous ancestor Violet as well as her offspring Auberon and the last generation before the end of what they refer to as The Tale. <br /><br />We see how the family started with a woman named Violet who can access the magic that the world has to offer but few others can use. We see how the family ends under the guidance of a long lost child who mysteriously vanished shortly after her birth.<br /><br />Characters have complex histories with each other and the story bounces back and forth along a timeline with very little notice or pretense. Simply put, this story is complicated.<br /><br />I enjoyed trying to decipher the layers of symbolism even if I sometimes fell short of fully understanding what Crowley was going for. At times it is clear that this book was written during a different era than my own. <br /><br />The novel feels like the kind of book I would have read back in school around the same time we were reading all of the other great white male authors of early Americana. I would love to see Little, Big on a curriculum alongside Faulker, Steinbeck, and Hemingway. He would fit right in and Little, Big is far more interesting than many of the other classics that are traditionally taught. I mean, this one has people who can talk to animals!<br /><br />Little, Big cries out for discussion. It needs to be dissected to be understood. It is not a surface level book and I believe that many readers will have many different interpretations of the events that unfold. If you've read it please tell me yours!</span>
  <a data-text-id="6586389410483700273" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_69250369" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/69250369" />
    <a title="Mosca" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1837675-mosca"><img alt="Mosca" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1363662463p2/1837675.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/69250369?book_show_action=true">Aug 28, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mosca" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mosca" href="/user/show/1837675-mosca">Mosca</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">patient readers</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1837675-mosca?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1837675-mosca?shelf=to-be-re-read">to-be-re-read</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer69250369" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8890146600812161151">---------------------------------------------------------------------<br/>The most readily evident characteristic of this book is the beautiful, almost musical prose that weaves throughout the telling of this â€œTaleâ€. The world created is seductive and at times dreamlike. The characters are so well introduced and sustained that you feel that they are good friends, even as you know their weaknesses.<br/><br/>For these reasons only, this book is worth the effort. But other reasons also abound.<br/><br/>Please, read this bo</span>
  <span id="freeText8890146600812161151" style="display:none">---------------------------------------------------------------------<br />The most readily evident characteristic of this book is the beautiful, almost musical prose that weaves throughout the telling of this â€œTaleâ€. The world created is seductive and at times dreamlike. The characters are so well introduced and sustained that you feel that they are good friends, even as you know their weaknesses.<br /><br />For these reasons only, this book is worth the effort. But other reasons also abound.<br /><br />Please, read this book slowly. This work feels Romantic, in the artistic sense. And the pace that may feel somnambulent to some, felt, to me, to be like a Chopin piano etudeâ€”a slow relaxing homage to the existence of the moment. Patient attention is required; but it is also rewarded. The reader's attention to the miracles of the moment sets the stage for the lifting of the veil. <br /><br />To say anything else about this book is more difficult for me. You may find blurbs concerning its plot and context by reading Goodreads book pages and the book backs. I do not want to further pigeon-hole this plot than it already has been because I feel that genre is usually irrelevant in quality literature.<br /><br />What I feel is important is the â€œTaleâ€ that is repeatedly referenced in this work. I'll try to explain:<br /><br />This book assumes the existence of another traditional world (Faerie), perhaps another half to our single world. <br /><br />Because I live in the rural Southwestern US, I have at least a superficial understanding of the â€œotherâ€ worlds intimately alive to the living native cultures and peoples of my adopted home. The traditional animate, â€œmagicalâ€ characteristics of the mountains, rivers, clouds, bears, trees, and all of creation is an accepted truth for many of my friends. My own long life has led me to similar experiences. This is not Fantasy. This is a global traditional worldview, shared by many traditional peoples on many continents today. In this book, the traditional European world of Faerie seems to best express that worldview.<br /><br />Back to the â€œTaleâ€. Many central characters in this book refer to a â€œTaleâ€ that is being lived in common with the beings from the â€œotherâ€ world. This â€œTaleâ€ may be a collaboration between beings â€œhereâ€ and beings â€œthereâ€ . Or this â€œTaleâ€ may be a wholly manufactured process from the world of Faerie. Whatever is true, this â€œTaleâ€ is central to the experience of this book.<br /><br />This â€œTaleâ€ is also central to my own confusion. In <i>Little, Big</i> there is an apparent frisson between this world and the world of Faerie. The resolution of that frisson seems central to this â€œTaleâ€ as well as to this book. <br /><br />However, as the book draws towards conclusion, there are many pieces connected; but many enigmas left as they are. Many resolutions reached; but many plot twists left untied. Herein lie my confusions. What are the conclusions? What now is known?<br /><br />But these inexpressible uncertainties (for me anyway) may well be the intended experience.<br /><br />Whatever is true, <i>Little,Big</i> is a masterful, complex, and lovingly beautiful work. I may reread it.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="8890146600812161151" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2412522194" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2412522194" />
    <a title="Tras" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/17620211-tras"><img alt="Tras" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1553624744p2/17620211.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2412522194?book_show_action=true">Jun 19, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tras" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tras" href="/user/show/17620211-tras">Tras</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2412522194" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15929357330511623912">I'm not going to try and write a detailed review of this book. I wouldn't even know where to begin. Though I suppose outlining how 'epic' the story is would be a decent starting point. 'Epic' in what sense? Well, it's between 500 and 600 pages for a start, depending on which edition you have, so it's not a light undertaking (literally!). But more importantly, it's epic in the way it recounts the Tale of the Drinkwater family, across four generations, spanning the 20th century, and a seemingly en</span>
  <span id="freeText15929357330511623912" style="display:none">I'm not going to try and write a detailed review of this book. I wouldn't even know where to begin. Though I suppose outlining how 'epic' the story is would be a decent starting point. 'Epic' in what sense? Well, it's between 500 and 600 pages for a start, depending on which edition you have, so it's not a light undertaking (literally!). But more importantly, it's epic in the way it recounts the Tale of the Drinkwater family, across four generations, spanning the 20th century, and a seemingly endless cast of characters. <br /><br />Sometimes, the Tale flows and you fool yourself into believing you have a firm grasp of proceedings. But you don't. And you won't. Not on the first reading anyway. Probably not even on the second or third. Upon completing the book, and comprehending that I'd barely even scratched the surface of understanding what I'd just read, I found myself reading some of the author's own - supposedly explanatory - notes on his own website. But they didn't explain much of anything really. If anything, they merely confused matters further. However, helpful readers pointed this out to Mr. Crowley, and he threw a few additional crumbs out to us mere partially-confused mortals. They helped a little.<br /><br />But whether you understand everything in its entirety, or you don't, it doesn't really matter since the actual Tale itself surpassses all of that. You will extract the essence of meaning from each chapter and, while you may feel like Smoky - lost in a sea of magical realism - the beauty of the writing, and enormous scope of John Crowley's imagination, will see you journey with Daily Alice, and Sophie, and Auberon, and Sylvie, and George Mouse, et al, into the welcoming unknown.<br /><br />Buckle up, it's gonna be a long ride!</span>
  <a data-text-id="15929357330511623912" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_302879" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/302879" />
    <a title="Melmoth" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/23734-melmoth"><img alt="Melmoth" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1368825642p2/23734.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/302879?book_show_action=true">Mar 17, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Melmoth" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Melmoth" href="/user/show/23734-melmoth">Melmoth</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/23734-melmoth?shelf=bk-bx">bk-bx</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer302879" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11093412292051198727">The writing in this book is incredible, and its depiction of the emotional reality of living in a large, tight-knit family is amazingly insightful and moving. <br/><br/><i>Little, Big</i> is giant in scope -- it tells the stories of several generations in this family and of the people they fall in love with (though it never loses its continuity or momentum). It's a fantasy novel, too, but the fairy tale elements mostly serve, I think, as a way to communicate both the enchantment and the burden of being part of a</span>
  <span id="freeText11093412292051198727" style="display:none">The writing in this book is incredible, and its depiction of the emotional reality of living in a large, tight-knit family is amazingly insightful and moving. <br /><br /><i>Little, Big</i> is giant in scope -- it tells the stories of several generations in this family and of the people they fall in love with (though it never loses its continuity or momentum). It's a fantasy novel, too, but the fairy tale elements mostly serve, I think, as a way to communicate both the enchantment and the burden of being part of a family that considers itself an institution, greater than the sum of its parts, where so much of the characters' identities derives from their membership and place within it. It also highlights the role of story-telling and myth in the creation and maintenance of such an emotional reality, a theme that pops up again in a subplot about a writing a soap-opera.<br /><br />This novel is engaging, artfully constructed, and very affective. I love it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11093412292051198727" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_539763" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/539763" />
    <a title="Erica" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/32722-erica"><img alt="Erica" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1175651798p2/32722.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/539763?book_show_action=true">Apr 02, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Erica" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Erica" href="/user/show/32722-erica">Erica</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer539763" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12604368698433069759">I read the last 20 or so pages of this late at night, half-asleep which puts your mind in the same state as the characters (characters getting lost in the woods, forgetting who they are, talking to animals - more in line with the fuzzy dreaming brain). Everything in the book was leading up to those last few pages. The Tale! When will it end!? What will happen to justify all these whispered anticipations for it? <br/><br/>After seeing reviews of the book on here, I picked it up with great anticipation. Mag</span>
  <span id="freeText12604368698433069759" style="display:none">I read the last 20 or so pages of this late at night, half-asleep which puts your mind in the same state as the characters (characters getting lost in the woods, forgetting who they are, talking to animals - more in line with the fuzzy dreaming brain). Everything in the book was leading up to those last few pages. The Tale! When will it end!? What will happen to justify all these whispered anticipations for it? <br /><br />After seeing reviews of the book on here, I picked it up with great anticipation. Magical Realism, I thought, my kind of genre. There is magic in it, but it's so murky, just as it is for the characters. I kept wondering if there was some metaphor to the events. By the end, I didn't know any better. There's a war being fought, but we don't see it and it doesn't last and it might not even have happened and the reasons for it are barely mentioned. Gar! That's too damn subtle! And a tangent about a president - an emperor who's somehow like a president and somehow not but we never visit the real world long enough to find out! The pieces of this puzzle are lovely and striking, but what does the finished product look like? A banquet in the woods? Why does this end for the characters have special meaning? It doesn't satisfy. Am I supposed to know something about gnosticism, as a previous reviewer mentioned? Where's the punch? What does it all mean!?!?! Haha.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12604368698433069759" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1146109" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1146109" />
    <a title="Katy Johnson" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/81990-katy-johnson"><img alt="Katy Johnson" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1178831078p2/81990.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1146109?book_show_action=true">May 10, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Katy Johnson" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Katy Johnson" href="/user/show/81990-katy-johnson">Katy Johnson</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1146109" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5028017924398871246">This book started out beautifully - it felt like a fantastical intermingling of "One Hundred Years of Solitude" and "Alice in Wonderland". The writing was exquisite. By the second half, though, the story became weird, difficult to follow, and basically unbelievable. I don't mind a little fantasy, but this was farfetched and terribly underdeveloped. Worth reading for the first half, but don't feel too bad if you put it down and never pick it up again. The magic that slowly builds and adds intrigu</span>
  <span id="freeText5028017924398871246" style="display:none">This book started out beautifully - it felt like a fantastical intermingling of "One Hundred Years of Solitude" and "Alice in Wonderland". The writing was exquisite. By the second half, though, the story became weird, difficult to follow, and basically unbelievable. I don't mind a little fantasy, but this was farfetched and terribly underdeveloped. Worth reading for the first half, but don't feel too bad if you put it down and never pick it up again. The magic that slowly builds and adds intrigue in the beginning turns out to be a pretty big let down in the end.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5028017924398871246" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer1349201971285900177">this is my favorite book. i'd go even further and say this book is an articulated facet of my soul. as such it's impossible to review. impossible! however i can speak a bit about the sense of it all...intensely dream like, complexly layered and other worldly in very transportive ways. ethereal. magical. earthy. twining and twisting. i've always equated this book to the Alice in Wonderland experience of falling down the rabbit hole. terrifying and unsettling, yes. but thrilling and mysterious and</span>
  <span id="freeText1349201971285900177" style="display:none">this is my favorite book. i'd go even further and say this book is an articulated facet of my soul. as such it's impossible to review. impossible! however i can speak a bit about the sense of it all...intensely dream like, complexly layered and other worldly in very transportive ways. ethereal. magical. earthy. twining and twisting. i've always equated this book to the Alice in Wonderland experience of falling down the rabbit hole. terrifying and unsettling, yes. but thrilling and mysterious and ultimately transformative as well. <br /><br />[*sigh* none of this does it justice, frankly.]</span>

//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('65x7AKa4DCQ4tcKGS1dRvK/jOXn+5OO/xzNVrvzakU5ILJw4trbibXckB34bolHGCONyyJNxCDeiIk/x65YStw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('ot9dxVy01FaWoxEAggXwLHURIz8I0172LNKJf5YRDnYBb7r9TLo6H9ky1PjS8PBW0hFojmVGtX5Jw5MggV2Njw==')})
  }
//]]>
</script>

<span id="freeTextContainer11324603171804253579"><i>Librarian Note: There is more than one author in the Goodreads database with this name. <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/topic/show_group/44217.Master_List_of_Author_Disambiguation_by_Spaces' rel='nofollow noopener'>See this thread for more information</a>.</i><br/><br/>John Crowley was born in Presque Isle, Maine, in 1942; his father was then an officer in the US Army Air Corps. He grew up in Vermont, northeastern Kentucky and (for the longest stretch) Indiana, where he went to high school and college. He moved to New York City after colle</span>
  <span id="freeText11324603171804253579" style="display:none"><i>Librarian Note: There is more than one author in the Goodreads database with this name. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/topic/show_group/44217.Master_List_of_Author_Disambiguation_by_Spaces" rel="nofollow noopener">See this thread for more information</a>.</i><br /><br />John Crowley was born in Presque Isle, Maine, in 1942; his father was then an officer in the US Army Air Corps. He grew up in Vermont, northeastern Kentucky and (for the longest stretch) Indiana, where he went to high school and college. He moved to New York City after college to make movies, and did find work in documentary films, an occupation he still pursues. He published his first novel (The Deep) in 1975, and his 15th volume of fiction (Endless Things) in 2007. Since 1993 he has taught creative writing at Yale University. In 1992 he received the Award in Literature from the American Academy and Institute of Arts and Letters.<br />His first published novels were science fiction: The Deep (1975) and Beasts (1976). Engine Summer (1979) was nominated for the 1980 American Book Award; it appears in David Pringleâ€™s 100 Best Science Fiction Novels.<br />In 1981 came Little, Big, which Ursula Le Guin described as a book that â€œall by itself calls for a redefinition of fantasy.â€<br />In 1980 Crowley embarked on an ambitious four-volume novel, Ã†gypt, comprising The Solitudes (originally published as Ã†gypt), Love &amp; Sleep, DÃ¦monomania, and Endless Things, published in May 2007. This series and Little, Big were cited when Crowley received the prestigious American Academy of Arts and Letters Award for Literature.<br />He is also the recipient of an Ingram Merrill Foundation grant. His recent novels are The Translator, recipient of the Premio Flaianno (Italy), and Lord Byronâ€™s Novel: The Evening Land, which contains an entire imaginary novel by the poet. A novella, The Girlhood of Shakespeare's Heroines, appeared in 2002. A museum-quality 25th anniversary edition of Little, Big, featuring the art of Peter Milton and a critical introduction by Harold Bloom, is in preparation.<br /><br /><i>Note:</i> The John Crowley who wrote <u>Sans Ã©pines, la rose: Tony Blair, un modÃ¨le pour l'Europe?</u> is a different author with the same name.
